<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1987 added the FNHRA to Title XIX of the Social Security Act, <em>i.e.</em>,<em> </em>Medicaid.  See 101 Stat. 1330-182.  The FNHRA added a materially identical section to the Medicare-focused portion of the Social Security Act, 101 Stat. 1330-160, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1395i-3 <em>et&nbsp;seq.</em>  Because Talevski's complaint relies only on the Medicaid-related FNHRA provisions, App. to Pet. for Cert. 76a, we hereafter follow the parties and the Court of Appeals in referring only to the pertinent Medicaid provisions, as codified in the U.&nbsp;S. Code (1396a(a)(28), 1396r).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"Nursing facility" is the FNHRA's term for a nursing home.  1396r(a).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Fourteenth Amendment hardly fails to secure 1983-enforceable rights because it directs state actors not to deny equal protection.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The "&nbsp;'rebuttable presumption'&nbsp;" that our cases describe, <em>Rancho Palos Verdes</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S., at 120, is not an artificially onerous court-made hurdle.  It merely reflects what 1983's plain text commands: The 1983 remedy is available to vindicate federal individual rights "secured by .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. la[w]."  In other words, the presumption recognizes that, even where Congress has unambiguously secured certain federal individual rights by law, it <em>may</em> have simultaneously given good reason (detectable with ordinary interpretive tools) to conclude that the 1983 remedy is not available for those rights, even though it "generally" is.  <em>Gonzaga</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S., at 284, and n.&nbsp;4.  And it also recognizes that it is the defendant's burden to show that a right otherwise secured by law is not 1983 enforceable.  <em>Rancho Palos Verdes</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S., at 120; <em>Gonzaga</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S., at 284, and n.&nbsp;4.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;We found similar (but not identical) language to be insufficient to preserve the presumption in <em>Middlesex County Sewerage Authority </em>v.<em> National Sea Clammers Assn.</em>,<em> </em>453 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1981),<em> </em>and <em>Rancho Palos Verdes</em> v. <em>Abrams</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S. 113 (2005).  See <em>id.</em>, at 125-127.  But those clauses did not purport to preserve "other remedies," and were embedded in statutes that (unlike the FNHRA) contained private judicial rights of action.  See <em>ibid.</em>; <em>Sea Clammers</em>, 453 U.&nbsp;S., at 6-7, 20, and n. 31.  The FNHRA's explicitly expressed objective of preserving other remedies bolsters our reluctance to infer implicit displacement of the 1983 remedy.  We think <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span>'s response in this regard overreads <em>Rancho Palos Verdes</em>.  See <em>post</em>, at 6.  That case merely provided an unremarkable description of <em>Sea Clammers</em>' "refus[al] to read" the saving clauses there as preserving 1983 actions, in light of the different (and significant) textual and contextual evidence of preclusion that the statutes at issue provided.  <em>Rancho Palos Verdes</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S., at 127. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The United States' suggestion that 1396r(h)(8) just ensures that remedies available under non-FNHRA, non-1983 statutes remain available is not persuasive.  Brief for United States as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> 34.  Nothing in the Act supports interpreting 1396r(h)(8)'s language in that manner, especially given 1983's unqualified command.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This survey merely scratches the surface of the requirements that FNHRA imposes upon participating States.  See also, <em>e.g.</em>, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1396r(e)(4) ("[T]he State must have implemented and enforced the nursing facility administrator standards developed" by the Secretary for Health and Human Services); 1396r(e)(7)(B)(i)(I) (in the case of mentally ill residents, "the State .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. must review and determine .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. whether or not the resident .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. requires the level of services provided by a nursing facility" or the services of different institutions).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The power of this tool has grown with the steady increase in federal income-tax revenues since the adoption of the Sixteenth Amendment.  Although the Revenue Act of 1913 accounted for only 10% of federal revenue, by 1950, the income tax was the Nation's largest source of revenue, and, by 2010, it accounted for a whopping 82% of federal revenue overall.  E. Jensen, Did the Sixteenth Amendment Ever Matter? Does It Matter Today? 108 Nw. U. L.&nbsp;Rev. 799, 807, n. 44 (2014).  This explosion of funds created unprecedented threats to federalism due to the increased use of grants.  Federal "[m]onetary grants, so-called grants-in-aid, became more frequent during the 1930's, and by 1950 they had reached $20 billion or 11.6% of state and local government expenditures."  <em>National Federation of Independent Business</em> v. <em>Sebelius</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S. 519, 674 (2012) (<em>NFIB</em>) (joint dissent of Scalia, Kennedy, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, JJ.) (citation and footnote omitted).  "By 1970 this number had grown to $123.7 billion or 29.1% of state and local government expenditures .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.  As of 2010, federal outlays to state and local governments came to over $608 billion or 37.5% of state and local government expenditures."  <em>Ibid. </em>(footnotes omitted).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Golden State Transit Corp.</em> v. <em>Los Angeles</em>, 493 U.&nbsp;S. 103, 106 (1989) ("[T]he plaintiff must assert the violation of a federal right. .&nbsp;.&nbsp;.  In deciding whether a federal right has been violated, we have considered whether the provision in question creates obligations binding on the governmental unit" and "whether the provision in question was intended to benefit the putative plaintiff&nbsp;" (alteration and internal quotation marks omitted)); <em>Pennhurst State School and Hospital</em> v. <em>Halderman</em>, 451 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 15, 16, n.&nbsp;12, 18-19 (1981) (repeatedly using the formula "rights and obligations" correlatively); see also W. Hohfeld, Some Fundamental Legal Conceptions as Applied in Judicial Reasoning, 23 Yale L.&nbsp;J. 16, 28-32 (1913).  This rule, it should be noted, addresses <em>what it means </em>for private rights to be secured by law, a distinct question from the level of clarity with which Congress must speak before courts may infer that it intended to create such rights.  See <em>Gonzaga Univ.</em> v. <em>Doe</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 273, 290 (2002).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Articles of Confederation granted Congress only the power to act upon States; it had no power to directly regulate individuals.  The Constitution flipped this arrangement by granting the Federal Government the power to regulate individuals directly, but not States.  See <em>New York </em>v.<em> United States</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 144, 162 (1992)<em> </em>("&nbsp;'The people, through [the Constitution], established a more perfect union by substituting a national government, acting, with ample power, directly upon the citizens, instead of the Confederate government, which acted with powers, greatly restricted, only upon the States'&nbsp;" (quoting <em>Lane County </em>v. <em>Oregon</em>, 7 Wall. 71, 76 (1869); emphasis deleted)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Congress possesses limited powers to directly regulate the States under the Reconstruction Amendments.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>City of Boerne </em>v. <em>Flores</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 507, 518 (1997).  Due to the federalism concerns inherent in such regulation, these enforcement powers are cabined by the congruence-and-proportionality test.  <em>Id.</em>, at 518-519.  The careful tailoring of this exception vividly proves the rule.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The anticommandeering doctrine protects "political subdivisions" of States against federal cooptation, as well as the States themselves.  <em>Printz </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 898, 935 (1997); see also <em>id.</em>, at 931, n.&nbsp;15 ("[T]he distinction in our Eleventh Amendment jurisprudence between States and municipalities is of no relevance here").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For this reason, the mere fact that spending conditions are enacted in&nbsp;statutory form is irrelevant; "not everything in a statute is legally binding."  Hamburger 131; see also D. Engdahl, The Contract Thesis of the Federal Spending Power, 52 S.&nbsp;D. L.&nbsp;Rev. 496, 500 (2007).  In other words, while Congress may influence policy "by attaching 'strings' to grants of money given to state and local governments, .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. those strings aren't <em>laws</em>," and do not secure rights, in the sense needed to support 1983 liability.  <em>United States </em>v. <em>Morgan</em>, 230 F.&nbsp;3d 1067, 1073 (CA8 2000) (Bye, J., specially concurring); see also <em>Westside Mothers</em> v. <em>Haveman</em>, 133 F. Supp. 2d 549, 581-582 (ED Mich. 2001) ("[N]o interest is 'secured' by the federal Medicaid statute.  Upon its enactment, this federal law does not vest in a single American the right or privilege of receiving federally-subsidized medical care. .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. [T]hough passed by both houses of Congress and signed by the President, the Medicaid statute has no force of its own.  It is only when a State .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. accepts the Federal Government's offer and agrees to participate in the program that any benefits accrue to eligible individuals"), rev'd, 289 F. 3d 852 (CA6 2002).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;One instance in particular demonstrates that the General Welfare Clause is simply a restriction on the Taxing Clause.  The Committee of Style slightly altered the text agreed to by the Convention by changing the comma after "Excises" into a semicolon, so that the General Welfare Clause "became an additional power, conjoined to the power to tax, rather than merely a limitation on it."  Hamburger 283.  The Convention, however, recognized the alteration and restored the comma, "corroborat[ing] the conclusion that the General Welfare Clause was not an independent power."  Natelson 28.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;After a debate on the Tucker Report, the House approved a resolution declaring Congress' authority to appropriate money to construct internal improvements pursuant to its enumerated powers, voting down several other resolutions that would have declared a congressional power to make monetary grants to States untethered to any enumerated power and that the Federal Government had the power to itself construct and maintain internal improvements.  32 Annals of Cong. 1381-1389 (1818).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p><em>&nbsp;</em>The<em> </em>anticoercion rule reflected in <em>Butler </em>remains a vital part of this Court's spending-power jurisprudence.  See <em>NFIB</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 575-585 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J.); <em>id.</em>, at 671-678 (joint dissent of Scalia, Kennedy, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, JJ.).  As <em>Butler </em>makes clear, that rule is firmly rooted in the contractual understanding of spending conditions, and <em>NFIB </em>further recognized the close connection between the rule and the anticommandeering doctrine when spending conditions involving States are at issue.  See <em>NFIB</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 575-585 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J.); <em>id.</em>, at 677-678 (joint dissent).  Indeed, the anticoercion rule supplements those doctrines through its recognition of the practical realities of Congress' modern spending power: Because the Federal Government's overwhelming fiscal resources enable it to create "gun to the head" situations in which there is no practical possibility of opting out, the rule prevents the Government from purchasing the States' regulatory powers to implement federal goals that it cannot attain through its own more limited powers.  <em>Id.</em>, at 581 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J.); accord, <em>id.</em>, at 677 (joint dissent) ("Congress effectively engages in this impermissible compulsion when state participation in a federal spending program is coerced, so that the States' choice whether to enact or administer a federal regulatory program is rendered illusory").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNopinion1.11" href="#FRopinion1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Justice Sutherland's dissent recognized that the majority had applied the traditional framework, disagreeing only with its interpretation of how the Social Security Act actually functioned.  See <em>Steward Machine Co.</em>,<em> </em>301 U.&nbsp;S., at 611-612.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNopinion1.12" href="#FRopinion1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;This civil rights connection was not arbitrary.  Section 1983 originated in the Enforcement Act of 1871, which Congress "passed for the express purpose of 'enforc[ing] the Provisions of the Fourteenth Amendment.'&nbsp;"  <em>Thiboutot</em>, 448 U.&nbsp;S., at 25, n.&nbsp;15 (Powell, J., dissenting) (quoting 17 Stat. 13; alteration in original).  Moreover, the original text of the statute referred only to rights "secured by the Constitution of the United States," 17 Stat. 13, the words "and laws" being added as part of the general 1874 revision of the federal statutes.  Rev. Stat. 1979, 42 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. 1983.  Under the circumstances, there is substantial reason to doubt that Congress fundamentally transformed a mechanism to enforce the Reconstruction Amendments into a freestanding right of action to remediate the violation of any federal statute, even those enacted beyond Congress' civil rights enforcement powers.  Importantly, if statutory 1983 actions were confined to laws enacted under Congress' Reconstruction Amendments enforcement powers--under which Congress may directly regulate States--the commandeering framework might apply differently--or not at all.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNopinion1.13" href="#FRopinion1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In dissent, Justice Powell set out the textual and historical case for interpreting 1983 to apply only to rights secured by laws enacted under Congress' enforcement powers.  <em>Thiboutot</em>, 448 U.&nbsp;S., at 11; see also <em>Chapman</em> v. <em>Houston Welfare Rights Organization</em>, 441 U.&nbsp;S. 600, 623 (1979) (Powell, J., concurring).  However, neither the Court, the parties, nor the dissent examined whether, even if they were considered "laws" for 1983 purposes, spending-power provisions could "secure" rights.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNopinion1.14" href="#FRopinion1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The court below had recognized but avoided the spending-power question by holding that Congress enacted the legislation at issue pursuant to its power to enforce the Fourteenth Amendment.  See <em>Halderman </em>v. <em>Pennhurst State School and Hospital</em>, 612 F.&nbsp;2d 84, 98 (CA3 1979) ("[W]e are not dealing with the implication of a private cause of action from a congressional enactment justified only by the spending power of the federal government, and we need not address the question whether such a statute could ever provide the predicate for private substantive rights. .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. Congress may, under section 5 [of the Fourteenth Amendment], establish certain restrictions that might otherwise implicate the prerogatives of the states").  The petitioners in <em>Pennhurst</em> squarely recognized that, if the legislation at issue was predicated on the spending power alone, "Congress exceeded the limits of that power."  Brief for Petitioners, O.T. 1980, No. 79-1404, etc., p.&nbsp;36, n. 57<em>.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Many litigants have recognized the constitutional problems.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Brief for Petitioner in <em>Gonzaga Univ. </em>v. <em>Doe</em>, O. T. 2001, No. 01-679, p. 42, n. 14 ("Nor is it clear that the conditions in Spending Clause legislation qualify as 'laws' under 1983.  Such conditions only become operative when the contract is accepted by a recipient; it is the resulting contract, not the federal legislation itself, that gives rise to obligations and allegedly enforceable rights"); Brief for Petitioner in <em>National Collegiate Athletic Assn. </em>v. <em>Smith</em>, O. T. 1998, No. 98-84, p.&nbsp;3;<em> </em>Brief for United States as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> in <em>Suter </em>v. <em>Artist M.</em>, O. T. 1991, No. 90-1488, p.&nbsp;12, n. 6.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNopinion1.16" href="#FRopinion1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Ironically, decades after it expressly disclaimed the pre-emptive effect of spending conditions in defending their constitutionality, see <em>supra</em>, at 26-28, the Federal Government argued the exact opposite in <em>Gonzaga</em>: "The Act of Congress establishing the program remains binding law with the full force and preemptive authority of federal legislation under the Supremacy Clause, and thus falls squarely within the 'laws' covered by Section 1983 and is fully capable of 'secur[ing]' rights."  Brief for United States as <em>Amicus Curiae</em> in No. 01-679, p. 19 (alteration in original).  With this reversal, the Government unwittingly argued that spending conditions are unconstitutional.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> <span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small>HEALTH AND HOSPITAL CORPORATION OF MARION COUNTY ET AL.</small></span> <em>v</em>. <span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small>TALEVSKI, AS PERSONAL REPRESENTATIVE OF THE ESTATE</small></span><span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small> OF</small></span><span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small> TALEVSKI</small></span> (2023)</h2>
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 21-806</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> November 08, 2022</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> June 08, 2023</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

  }

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })

      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search

          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {

              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');

              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);

          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {

      });
